<p><b>Henry Ford</b> (July 30, 1863 – April 7, 1947) was an American <a href="/wiki/Technological_and_industrial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Technological and industrial history of the United States">industrialist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Business_magnate" title="Business magnate">business magnate</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>, and chief developer of the <a href="/wiki/Assembly_line" title="Assembly line">assembly line</a> technique of <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a>. By creating the first automobile that middle-class Americans could afford, he converted the automobile from an expensive luxury into an accessible conveyance that profoundly impacted the landscape of the 20th century.
</p><p>His introduction of the Ford <a href="/wiki/Ford_Model_T" title="Ford Model T">Model T</a> automobile revolutionized transportation and American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industry_(manufacturing)" title="Industry (manufacturing)">industry</a>. As the Ford Motor Company owner, he became one of the richest and best-known people in the world. He is credited with "<a href="/wiki/Fordism" title="Fordism">Fordism</a>", the mass production of inexpensive goods coupled with high wages for workers. Ford had a global vision, with <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a> as the key to peace. His intense commitment to systematically lowering costs resulted in many technical and business innovations, including a <a href="/wiki/Franchising" title="Franchising">franchise</a> system that put dealerships throughout North America and major cities on six continents. Ford left most of his vast wealth to the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Foundation" title="Ford Foundation">Ford Foundation</a> and arranged for his family to permanently control it.
</p><p>Ford was also widely known for his <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> during the first years of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, and later for promoting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antisemitic" title="Antisemitic">antisemitic</a> content, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protocols_of_the_Elders_of_Zion" title="The Protocols of the Elders of Zion">The Protocols of the Elders of Zion</a></i>, through his newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dearborn_Independent" title="The Dearborn Independent">The Dearborn Independent</a></i>, and the book <i><a href="/wiki/The_International_Jew" title="The International Jew">The International Jew</a></i>.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Henry Ford was born July 30, 1863, on a farm in <a href="/wiki/Springwells_Township,_Michigan" title="Springwells Township, Michigan">Springwells Township, Michigan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> His father, William Ford (1826–1905), was born in <a href="/wiki/County_Cork" title="County Cork">County Cork</a>, Ireland, to a family that had emigrated from <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a>, England in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> His mother, Mary Ford (née Litogot; 1839–1876), was born in Michigan as the youngest child of <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Americans" title="Belgian Americans">Belgian</a> immigrants; her parents died when she was a child and she was adopted by neighbors, the O'Herns. Henry Ford's siblings were Margaret Ford (1867–1938); Jane Ford (c. 1868–1945); William Ford (1871–1917) and Robert Ford (1873–1934).  Ford finished eighth grade at a one room school,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Springwells Middle School. He never attended high school; he later took a bookkeeping course at a commercial school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>His father gave him a pocket watch when he was 12. At 15, Ford dismantled and reassembled the timepieces of friends and neighbors dozens of times, gaining the reputation of a watch repairman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> At twenty, Ford walked four miles to their <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopal</a> church every Sunday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford was devastated when his mother died in 1876. His father expected him to take over the family farm eventually, but he despised farm work. He later wrote, "I never had any particular love for the farm—it was the mother on the farm I loved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1879, Ford left home to work as an apprentice <a href="/wiki/Machinist" title="Machinist">machinist</a> in Detroit, first with James F. Flower &amp; Bros., and later with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit_Dry_Dock" title="Detroit Dry Dock">Detroit Dry Dock</a> Co. In 1882, he returned to Dearborn to work on the family farm, where he became adept at operating the <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Farm_Engine" title="Westinghouse Farm Engine">Westinghouse portable steam engine</a>. He was later hired by <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Corporation" title="Westinghouse Electric Corporation">Westinghouse</a> to service their steam engines. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford stated two significant events occurred in 1875 when he was 12. He received the watch, and he witnessed the operation of a <a href="/wiki/Nichols_and_Shepard" title="Nichols and Shepard">Nichols and Shepard</a> road engine, "...the first vehicle other than horse-drawn that I had ever seen". In his farm workshop, Ford built a "steam wagon or tractor" and a steam car, but thought "steam was not suitable for light vehicles," as "the boiler was dangerous." Ford also said that he "did not see the use of experimenting with electricity, due to the expense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trolley_wires" title="Trolley wires">trolley wires</a>, and "no storage battery was in sight of a weight that was practical." In 1885, Ford repaired an <a href="/wiki/Otto_engine" title="Otto engine">Otto engine</a>, and in 1887 he built a four-cycle model with a one-inch <a href="/wiki/Bore_(engine)" title="Bore (engine)">bore</a> and a three-inch <a href="/wiki/Stroke_(engine)" title="Stroke (engine)">stroke</a>. In 1890, Ford started work on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_cylinder" title="Two cylinder">two-cylinder</a> engine. 
</p><p>Ford stated, "In 1892, I completed my first motor car, powered by a two-cylinder four <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">horsepower</a> motor, with a two-and-half-inch bore and a six-inch stroke, which was connected to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countershaft" title="Countershaft">countershaft</a> by a belt and then to the rear wheel by a chain. The belt was shifted by a <a href="/wiki/Clutch" title="Clutch">clutch lever</a> to control speeds at 10 or 20 <a href="/wiki/Miles_per_hour" title="Miles per hour">miles per hour</a>, augmented by a <a href="/wiki/Throttle" title="Throttle">throttle</a>. Other features included 28-inch wire bicycle wheels with rubber tires, a foot brake, a 3-gallon gasoline tank, and later, a water jacket around the cylinders for cooling. Ford added that "in the spring of 1893 the machine was running to my partial satisfaction and giving an opportunity further to test out the design and material on the road." Between 1895 and 1896, Ford drove that machine about 1000 miles. He then started a second car in 1896, eventually building three of them in his home workshop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Ford_1888.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1867" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Henry_Ford_1888.jpg/170px-Henry_Ford_1888.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Henry_Ford_1888.jpg/255px-Henry_Ford_1888.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Henry_Ford_1888.jpg/340px-Henry_Ford_1888.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Ford_1888.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry Ford in 1888<br/> (aged 25)</div></div></div>
<p>Ford married <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clara_Jane_Bryant" title="Clara Jane Bryant">Clara Jane Bryant</a> (1866–1950) on April 11, 1888, and supported himself by farming and running a sawmill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> They had one child, <a href="/wiki/Edsel_Ford" title="Edsel Ford">Edsel Ford</a> (1893–1943).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<p>In 1891, Ford became an engineer with the <a href="/wiki/DTE_Electric_Company" title="DTE Electric Company">Edison Illuminating Company of Detroit</a>. After his promotion to Chief Engineer in 1893, he had enough time and money to devote attention to his experiments on gasoline engines. These experiments culminated in 1896 with the completion of a self-propelled vehicle, which he named the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Quadricycle" title="Ford Quadricycle">Ford Quadricycle</a>. He test-drove it on June 4. After various test drives, Ford brainstormed ways to improve the Quadricycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 1896, Ford attended a meeting of Edison executives, where he was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a>. Edison approved of Ford's automobile experimentation. Encouraged by Edison, Ford designed and built a second vehicle, completing it in 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup> Backed by the capital of Detroit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lumber_baron" title="Lumber baron">lumber baron</a> William H. Murphy, Ford resigned from the Edison Company and founded the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Automobile_Company" title="Detroit Automobile Company">Detroit Automobile Company</a> on August 5, 1899.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup> However, the automobiles produced were of a lower quality and higher price than Ford wanted. Ultimately, the company was not successful and was dissolved in January 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the help of <a href="/wiki/C._Harold_Wills" title="C. Harold Wills">C. Harold Wills</a>, Ford designed, built, and successfully raced a 26-horsepower automobile in October 1901. With this success, Murphy and other stockholders in the Detroit Automobile Company formed the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford_Company" title="Henry Ford Company">Henry Ford Company</a> on November 30, 1901, with Ford as chief engineer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1902, Murphy brought in <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Leland" title="Henry M. Leland">Henry M. Leland</a> as a consultant; Ford, in response, left the company bearing his name. With Ford gone, Leland renamed the company the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadillac_Automobile_Company" title="Cadillac Automobile Company">Cadillac Automobile Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Teaming up with former racing cyclist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tom_Cooper_(driver)" title="Tom Cooper (driver)">Tom Cooper</a>, Ford also produced the 80+ horsepower racer "999," which <a href="/wiki/Barney_Oldfield" title="Barney Oldfield">Barney Oldfield</a> was to drive to victory in a race in October 1902. Ford received the backing of an old acquaintance, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Y._Malcomson" title="Alexander Y. Malcomson">Alexander Y. Malcomson</a>, a Detroit-area coal dealer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup> They formed a partnership, "Ford &amp; Malcomson, Ltd." to manufacture automobiles. Ford went to work designing an inexpensive automobile, and the duo leased a factory and contracted with a machine shop owned by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_F._Dodge" title="John F. Dodge">John</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horace_E._Dodge" title="Horace E. Dodge">Horace E. Dodge</a> to supply over $160,000 in parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup> Sales were slow, and a crisis arose when the Dodge brothers demanded payment for their first shipment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ford_Motor_Company">Ford Motor Company</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="862" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/170px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/255px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg/340px-Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Edison_Firestone1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry Ford with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvey_Firestone" title="Harvey Firestone">Harvey Firestone</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myers,_Florida" title="Fort Myers, Florida">Fort Myers, Florida</a>, February 11, 1929.</div></div></div>
<p>In response, Malcomson brought in another group of investors and convinced the Dodge Brothers to accept a portion of the new company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup> Ford &amp; Malcomson was reincorporated as the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> on June 16, 1903,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birth_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-birth-13">[13]</a></sup> with $28,000 capital. The original investors included Ford and Malcomson, the Dodge brothers, Malcomson's uncle <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_S._Gray_(Michigan)" title="John S. Gray (Michigan)">John S. Gray</a>, Malcolmson's secretary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Couzens" title="James Couzens">James Couzens</a>, and two of Malcomson's lawyers, John W. Anderson and <a href="/wiki/Horace_Rackham" title="Horace Rackham">Horace Rackham</a>. Because of Ford's volatility, Gray was elected president of the company. Ford then demonstrated a newly designed car on the ice of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Saint_Clair_(North_America)" title="Lake Saint Clair (North America)">Lake St. Clair</a>, driving 1 mile (1.6 km) in 39.4 seconds and setting a new <a href="/wiki/Land_speed_record" title="Land speed record">land speed record</a> at 91.3 miles per hour (146.9 kilometres per hour). Convinced by this success, race driver <a href="/wiki/Barney_Oldfield" title="Barney Oldfield">Barney Oldfield</a>, who named this new Ford model "<a href="/wiki/Ford_999" title="Ford 999">999</a>" in honor of the fastest locomotive of the day, took the car around the country, making the Ford brand known throughout the United States. Ford also was one of the early backers of the <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_500" title="Indianapolis 500">Indianapolis 500</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Model_T">Model T</span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Model_T" title="Model T">Model T</a> debuted on October 1, 1908. It had the steering wheel on the left, which every other company soon copied. The entire engine and transmission were enclosed; the four cylinders were cast in a solid block; the suspension used two semi-elliptic springs. The car was very simple to drive, and easy and cheap to repair. It was so cheap at $825 in 1908 ($24,880 today), with the price falling every year, that by the 1920s, a majority of American drivers had learned to drive on the Model T,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> despite the fact that drivers who were only familiar with the Model T's unique foot-operated planetary transmission and steering-column operated throttle-cum-accelerator had to learn a completely different set of skills to drive any other gasoline-powered automobile of the time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2826" data-file-width="3916" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg/220px-Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg/330px-Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg/440px-Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ford_assembly_line_-_1913.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ford assembly line, 1913</div></div></div>
<p>Ford created a huge publicity machine in Detroit to ensure every newspaper carried stories and ads about the new product. Ford's network of local dealers made the car ubiquitous in almost every city in North America. As independent dealers, the franchises grew rich and publicized not just the Ford but also the concept of automobiling; local motor clubs sprang up to help new drivers and encourage them to explore the countryside. Ford was always eager to sell to farmers, who looked at the vehicle as a commercial device to help their business. Sales skyrocketed—several years posted 100% gains on the previous year. In 1913, Ford introduced moving assembly belts into his plants, which enabled an enormous increase in production. Although Ford is often credited with the idea, contemporary sources indicate that the concept and development came from employees <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clarence_Avery" title="Clarence Avery">Clarence Avery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_E._Martin" title="Peter E. Martin">Peter E. Martin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_E._Sorensen" title="Charles E. Sorensen">Charles E. Sorensen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/C._Harold_Wills" title="C. Harold Wills">C. Harold Wills</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> (See <a href="/wiki/Ford_Piquette_Avenue_Plant" title="Ford Piquette Avenue Plant">Ford Piquette Avenue Plant</a>)
</p><p>Sales passed 250,000 in 1914. By 1916, as the price dropped to $360 for the basic touring car, sales reached 472,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1918, half of all cars in the United States were Model Ts. All new cars were black; as Ford wrote in his autobiography, "Any customer can have a car painted any color that he wants so long as it is black."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_Crowther_1922_p72_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford_Crowther_1922_p72-18">[18]</a></sup> Until the development of the assembly line, which mandated black because of its quicker drying time, Model Ts were available in other colors, including red. The design was fervently promoted and defended by Ford, and production continued as late as 1927; the final total production was 15,007,034. This record stood for the next 45 years, and was achieved in 19 years from the introduction of the first Model T (1908).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry Ford turned the presidency of Ford Motor Company over to his son Edsel Ford in December 1918. Henry retained final decision authority and sometimes reversed the decisions of his son. Ford started another company, Henry Ford and Son, and made a show of taking himself and his best employees to the new company; the goal was to scare the remaining holdout stockholders of the Ford Motor Company to sell their stakes to him before they lost most of their value. (He was determined to have full control over strategic decisions.) The ruse worked, and Ford and Edsel purchased all remaining stock from the other investors, thus giving the family sole ownership of the company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1922, Ford also purchased <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Motor_Company" title="Lincoln Motor Company">Lincoln Motor Co.</a>, founded by Cadillac founder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Leland" title="Henry Leland">Henry Leland</a> and his son Wilfred during World War I. The Lelands briefly stayed to manage the company, but were soon expelled from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Despite this acquisition of a premium car maker, Henry displayed relatively little enthusiasm for luxury automobiles in contrast to Edsel, who actively sought to expand Ford into the upscale market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The original <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_L_series" title="Lincoln L series">Lincoln Model L</a> that the Lelands had introduced in 1920 was also kept in production, untouched for a decade until it became too outdated. It was replaced by the modernized <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_K_series" title="Lincoln K series">Model K</a> in 1931.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster,_Coimbatore_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster%2C_Coimbatore_%281%29.jpg/220px-1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster%2C_Coimbatore_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster%2C_Coimbatore_%281%29.jpg/330px-1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster%2C_Coimbatore_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster%2C_Coimbatore_%281%29.jpg/440px-1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster%2C_Coimbatore_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1924-1925_Ford_-_T_Roadster,_Coimbatore_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1926 Ford T Roadster on display in India</div></div></div>
<p>By the mid-1920s, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> was rapidly rising as the leading American automobile manufacturer. GM president <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Sloan" title="Alfred Sloan">Alfred Sloan</a> established the company's "price ladder" whereby GM would offer an automobile for "every purse and purpose" in contrast to Ford's lack of interest in anything outside the low-end market. Although Henry Ford was against replacing the Model T, now 16 years old, Chevrolet was mounting a bold new challenge as GM's entry-level division in the company's price ladder. Ford also resisted the increasingly popular idea of payment plans for cars. With Model T sales starting to slide, Ford was forced to relent and approve work on a successor model, shutting down production for 18 months. During this time, Ford constructed a massive new assembly plant at River Rouge for the new Model A, which launched in 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to its price ladder, GM also quickly established itself at the forefront of automotive styling under <a href="/wiki/Harley_Earl" title="Harley Earl">Harley Earl</a>'s Arts &amp; Color Department, another area of automobile design that Henry Ford did not entirely appreciate or understand. Ford would not have a true equivalent of the GM styling department for many years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Model_A_and_Ford.27s_later_career"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Model_A_and_Ford's_later_career">Model A and Ford's later career</span></h4>
<p>By 1926, flagging sales of the Model T finally convinced Ford to make a new model. He pursued the project with a great deal of interest in the design of the engine, chassis, and other mechanical necessities, while leaving the body design to his son. Although Ford fancied himself an engineering genius, he had little formal training in mechanical engineering and could not even read a blueprint. A talented team of engineers performed most of the actual work of designing the Model A (and later the flathead V8) with Ford supervising them closely and giving them overall direction. Edsel also managed to prevail over his father's initial objections in the inclusion of a sliding-shift transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956p223_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956p223-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The result was the successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford_Model_A_(1927-1931)" title="Ford Model A (1927-1931)">Ford Model A</a>, introduced in December 1927 and produced through 1931, with a total output of more than four million. Subsequently, the Ford company adopted an annual model change system similar to that recently pioneered by its competitor General Motors (and still in use by automakers today). Not until the 1930s did Ford overcome his objection to finance companies, and the Ford-owned <a href="/wiki/Universal_Credit_Corporation" title="Universal Credit Corporation">Universal Credit Corporation</a> became a major car-financing operation. Henry Ford still resisted many technological innovations such as hydraulic brakes and all-metal roofs, which Ford vehicles did not adopt until 1935–36. For 1932 however, Ford dropped a bombshell with the flathead Ford V8, the first low-price eight-cylinder engine. The flathead V8, variants of which were used in Ford vehicles for 20 years, was the result of a secret project launched in 1930 and Henry had initially considered a radical X-8 engine before agreeing to a conventional design. It gave Ford a reputation as a performance make well-suited for hot-rodding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford did not believe in accountants; he amassed one of the world's largest fortunes without ever having his company <a href="/wiki/Audit" title="Audit">audited</a> under his administration. Without an accounting department, Ford had no way of knowing exactly how much money was being taken in and spent each month, and the company's bills and invoices were reportedly guessed at by weighing them on a scale.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Not until 1956 would Ford be a publicly-traded company.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Also, at Edsel's insistence, Ford launched Mercury in 1939 as a mid-range make to challenge Dodge and Buick, although Henry also displayed relatively little enthusiasm for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_philosophy">Labor philosophy</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Five-dollar_wage">Five-dollar wage</span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timehenryford.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Timehenryford.jpg/170px-Timehenryford.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Timehenryford.jpg/255px-Timehenryford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Timehenryford.jpg/340px-Timehenryford.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timehenryford.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine, January 14, 1935</div></div></div>
<p>Ford was a pioneer of "<a href="/wiki/Welfare_capitalism" title="Welfare capitalism">welfare capitalism</a>", designed to improve the lot of his workers and especially to reduce the heavy <a href="/wiki/Turnover_(employment)" title="Turnover (employment)">turnover</a> that had many departments hiring 300 men per year to fill 100 slots. Efficiency meant hiring and keeping the best workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford astonished the world in 1914 by offering a $5 per day wage ($140 today), which more than doubled the rate of most of his workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland, Ohio</a>, newspaper editorialized that the announcement "shot like a blinding rocket through the dark clouds of the present industrial depression".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The move proved extremely profitable; instead of constant employee turnover, the best mechanics in Detroit flocked to Ford, bringing their <a href="/wiki/Human_capital" title="Human capital">human capital</a> and expertise, raising productivity, and lowering training costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Ford announced his $5-per-day program on January 5, 1914, raising the minimum daily pay from $2.34 to $5 for qualifying male workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Detroit was already a high-wage city, but competitors were forced to raise wages or lose their best workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Ford's policy proved that paying employees more would enable them to afford the cars they were producing and thus boost the local economy. He viewed the increased wages as profit-sharing linked with rewarding those who were most productive and of good character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_Crowther_1922_pp126-130_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford_Crowther_1922_pp126-130-35">[35]</a></sup> It may have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Couzens" title="James Couzens">Couzens</a> who convinced Ford to adopt the $5-day wage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Real profit-sharing was offered to employees who had worked at the company for six months or more, and, importantly, conducted their lives in a manner of which Ford's "Social Department" approved. They frowned on heavy drinking, gambling, and on what are now called <a href="/wiki/Deadbeat_parent" title="Deadbeat parent">deadbeat dads</a>. The Social Department used 50 investigators and support staff to maintain employee standards; a large percentage of workers were able to qualify for this "profit-sharing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford's incursion into his employees' private lives was highly controversial, and he soon backed off from the most intrusive aspects. By the time he wrote his 1922 memoir, he had spoken of the Social Department and the private conditions for profit-sharing in the past tense. He admitted that "paternalism has no place in the industry. Welfare work that consists in prying into employees' private concerns is out of date. Men need counsel and men need help, often special help; and all this ought to be rendered for decency's sake. But the broad workable plan of investment and participation will do more to solidify the industry and strengthen the organization than will any social work on the outside. Without changing the principle we have changed the method of payment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_Crowther_1922_p130_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford_Crowther_1922_p130-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Five-day_workweek">Five-day workweek</span></h5>
<p>In addition to raising his workers' wages, Ford also introduced a new, reduced workweek in 1926. The decision was made in 1922, when Ford and Crowther described it as six 8-hour days, giving a 48-hour week,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_Crowther_1922_p126_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford_Crowther_1922_p126-39">[39]</a></sup> but in 1926 it was announced as five 8-hour days, giving a 40-hour week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The program apparently started with Saturday being designated a workday, before becoming a day off sometime later. On May 1, 1926, the Ford Motor Company's factory workers switched to a five-day, 40-hour workweek, with the company's office workers making the transition the following August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford had decided to boost productivity, as workers were expected to put more effort into their work in exchange for more leisure time. Ford also believed decent leisure time was good for business, giving workers additional time to purchase and consume more goods. However, charitable concerns also played a role. Ford explained, "It is high time to rid ourselves of the notion that leisure for workmen is either 'lost time' or a class privilege."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_unions">Labor unions</span></h5>
<p>Ford was adamantly against <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">labor unions</a>. He explained his views on unions in Chapter 18 of <i>My Life and Work</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_Crowther_1922_pp253-266_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford_Crowther_1922_pp253-266-42">[42]</a></sup> He thought they were too heavily influenced by leaders who would end up doing more harm than good for workers despite their ostensible good motives. <span class="anchor" id="No_prosperity_without_productivity"></span> Most wanted to restrict productivity as a means to foster employment, but Ford saw this as self-defeating because, in his view, productivity was necessary for economic prosperity to exist.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>He believed that productivity gains that obviated certain jobs would nevertheless stimulate the broader economy and grow new jobs elsewhere, whether within the same corporation or in others. Ford also believed that union leaders had a <a href="/wiki/Perverse_incentive" title="Perverse incentive">perverse incentive</a> to foment perpetual socio-economic crises to maintain their power. Meanwhile, he believed that smart managers had an incentive to do right by their workers, because doing so would maximize their profits. However, Ford did acknowledge that many managers were basically too bad at managing to understand this fact. But Ford believed that eventually, if good managers such as he could fend off the attacks of misguided people from both left and right (i.e., both socialists and bad-manager reactionaries), the good managers would create a socio-economic system wherein neither bad management nor bad unions could find enough support to continue existing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>To forestall union activity, Ford promoted <a href="/wiki/Harry_Bennett" title="Harry Bennett">Harry Bennett</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a> boxer, to head the Service Department. Bennett employed various intimidation tactics to quash union organizing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> On March 7, 1932, during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, unemployed Detroit auto workers staged the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Hunger_March" title="Ford Hunger March">Ford Hunger March</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford_River_Rouge_Complex" title="Ford River Rouge Complex">Ford River Rouge Complex</a> to present 14 demands to Henry Ford. The <a href="/wiki/Dearborn,_Michigan" title="Dearborn, Michigan">Dearborn</a> police department and Ford security guards opened fire on workers leading to over sixty injuries and five deaths. On May 26, 1937, Bennett's security men beat members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a> (UAW), including <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reuther" title="Walter Reuther">Walter Reuther</a>, with clubs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup> While Bennett's men were beating the UAW representatives, the supervising police chief on the scene was Carl Brooks, an alumnus of Bennett's Service Department, and [Brooks] "did not give orders to intervene".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The following day photographs of the injured UAW members appeared in newspapers, later becoming known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_the_Overpass" title="The Battle of the Overpass">The Battle of the Overpass</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the late 1930s and early 1940s, Edsel—who was president of the company—thought Ford had to come to a <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> agreement with the unions because the violence, work disruptions, and bitter stalemates could not go on forever. But Ford, who still had the final veto in the company on a <i>de facto</i> basis even if not an official one, refused to cooperate. For several years, he kept Bennett in charge of talking to the unions trying to organize the Ford Motor Company. Sorensen's memoir<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956p261_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956p261-46">[46]</a></sup> makes clear that Ford's purpose in putting Bennett in charge was to make sure no agreements were ever reached.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Ford Motor Company was the last Detroit automaker to recognize the UAW, despite pressure from the rest of the U.S. automotive industry and even the U.S. government. A sit-down strike by the UAW union in April 1941 closed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Rouge_Plant" title="River Rouge Plant">River Rouge Plant</a>. Sorensen recounted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956pp266-272_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956pp266-272-47">[47]</a></sup> that a distraught Henry Ford was very close to following through with a threat to break up the company rather than cooperate. Still, his wife Clara told him she would leave him if he destroyed the family business. In her view, it would not be worth the chaos it would create. Ford complied with his wife's ultimatum and even agreed with her in retrospect. Overnight, the Ford Motor Company went from the most stubborn holdout among automakers to the one with the most favorable UAW contract terms. The contract was signed in June 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956pp266-272_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956pp266-272-47">[47]</a></sup> About a year later, Ford told Walter Reuther, "It was one of the most sensible things Harry Bennett ever did when he got the UAW into this plant." Reuther inquired, "What do you mean?" Ford replied, "Well, you've been fighting General Motors and the Wall Street crowd. Now you're in here and we've given you a union shop and more than you got out of them. That puts you on our side, doesn't it? We can fight General Motors and Wall Street together, eh?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ford_Airplane_Company">Ford Airplane Company</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Henry_Ford&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2017</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Like other automobile companies, Ford entered the aviation business during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, building <a href="/wiki/Liberty_L-12" title="Liberty L-12">Liberty engines</a>. After the war, it returned to auto manufacturing until 1925, when Ford acquired the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stout_Metal_Airplane_Division_of_the_Ford_Motor_Company" title="Stout Metal Airplane Division of the Ford Motor Company">Stout Metal Airplane Company</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ford_4ATF.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="680" decoding="async" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ford_4ATF.jpg/220px-Ford_4ATF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ford_4ATF.jpg/330px-Ford_4ATF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ford_4ATF.jpg/440px-Ford_4ATF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ford_4ATF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ford 4-AT-F (EC-RRA) of the Spanish Republican Airline, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L.A.P.E." title="L.A.P.E.">L.A.P.E.</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ford's most successful aircraft was the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Trimotor" title="Ford Trimotor">Ford 4AT Trimotor</a>, often called the "Tin Goose" because of its corrugated metal construction. It used a new alloy called <a href="/wiki/Alclad" title="Alclad">Alclad</a> that combined the corrosion resistance of aluminum with the strength of <a href="/wiki/Duralumin" title="Duralumin">duralumin</a>. The plane was similar to <a href="/wiki/Fokker" title="Fokker">Fokker</a>'s V.VII-3m, and some say<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2021)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> that Ford's engineers surreptitiously measured the Fokker plane and then copied it. The Trimotor first flew on June 11, 1926, and was the first successful U.S. passenger airliner, accommodating about 12 passengers in a rather uncomfortable fashion. Several variants were also used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> has honored Ford for changing the aviation industry. 199 Trimotors were built before it was discontinued in 1933, when the Ford Airplane Division shut down because of poor sales during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.
</p><p><br/>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I_era">World War I era</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Peace_Ship" title="Peace Ship">Peace Ship</a> and <a href="/wiki/1918_United_States_Senate_election_in_Michigan" title="1918 United States Senate election in Michigan">1918 United States Senate election in Michigan</a></div>
<p>Ford opposed war, which he viewed as a terrible waste,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford-Bio-A&amp;E_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford-Bio-A&amp;E-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and supported causes that opposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_intervention" title="Military intervention">military intervention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Ford became highly critical of those who he felt financed war, and he tried to stop them. In 1915, the pacifist <a href="/wiki/Rosika_Schwimmer" title="Rosika Schwimmer">Rosika Schwimmer</a> gained favor with Ford, who agreed to fund a <a href="/wiki/Peace_Ship" title="Peace Ship">Peace Ship</a> to Europe, where World War I was raging. He led 170 other peace activists. Ford's Episcopalian pastor, Reverend Samuel S. Marquis, accompanied him on the mission. Marquis headed Ford's Sociology Department from 1913 to 1921. Ford talked to President Woodrow Wilson about the mission but had no government support. His group went to neutral Sweden and the Netherlands to meet with peace activists. A target of much ridicule, Ford left the ship as soon as it reached Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In 1915, Ford blamed "German-Jewish bankers" for instigating the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to biographer Steven Watts, Ford's status as a leading industrialist gave him a worldview that warfare was wasteful folly that retarded long-term economic growth. The losing side in the war typically suffered heavy damage. Small business were especially hurt, for it takes years to recuperate. He argued in many newspaper articles that a focus on business efficiency would discourage warfare because,  “If every man who manufactures an article would make the very best he can in the very best way at the very lowest possible price the world would be kept out of war, for commercialists would not have to search for outside markets which the other fellow covets.”  Ford admitted that munitions makers enjoyed wars, but he argued the most businesses wanted to avoid wars and instead work to manufacture and sell useful goods, hire workers, and generate steady long-term profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford's British factories produced <a href="/wiki/Fordson" title="Fordson">Fordson</a> tractors to increase the British food supply, as well as trucks and warplane engines. When the U.S. entered the war in 1917, Ford went quiet on foreign policy. His company became a major supplier of weapons, especially the Liberty engine for warplanes and <a href="/wiki/Eagle-class_patrol_craft" title="Eagle-class patrol craft">anti-submarine boats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ford-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 95–100, 119">: 95–100, 119 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1918, with the war on and the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> a growing issue in global politics, President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, a Democrat, encouraged Ford to run for a Michigan seat in the U.S. Senate. Wilson believed that Ford could tip the scales in Congress in favor of Wilson's proposed <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League</a>. "You are the only man in Michigan who can be elected and help bring about the peace you so desire," the president wrote Ford. Ford wrote back: "If they want to elect me let them do so, but I won't make a penny's investment." Ford did run, however, and came within 7,000 votes of winning, out of more than 400,000 cast statewide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> He was defeated in a close election by the Republican candidate, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Truman_Newberry" title="Truman Newberry">Truman Newberry</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">United States Secretary of the Navy</a>. Ford remained a staunch Wilsonian and supporter of the League. When Wilson made a major speaking tour in the summer of 1919 to promote the League, Ford helped fund the attendant publicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Coming_of_World_War_II_and_Ford.27s_mental_collapse"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Coming_of_World_War_II_and_Ford's_mental_collapse">Coming of World War II and Ford's mental collapse</span></h4>
<p>Ford had opposed the United States' entry into World War II<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and continued to believe that international business could generate the prosperity that would head off wars. Ford "insisted that war was the product of greedy financiers who sought profit in human destruction". In 1939, he went so far as to claim that the torpedoing of U.S. merchant ships by German submarines was the result of conspiratorial activities undertaken by financier war-makers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The financiers to whom he was referring was Ford's code for Jews; he had also accused Jews of fomenting the First World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In the run-up to World War II and when the war erupted in 1939, he reported that he did not want to trade with belligerents. Like many other businessmen of the Great Depression era, he never liked or entirely trusted the Franklin Roosevelt Administration, and thought Roosevelt was inching the U.S. closer to war. Ford continued to do business with <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, including the manufacture of war <a href="/wiki/Materiel" title="Materiel">materiel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup> However, he also agreed to build warplane engines for the British government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In early 1940, he boasted that Ford Motor Company would soon be able to produce 1,000 U.S. warplanes a day, even though it did not have an aircraft production facility at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LegendOfHenryFord_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-LegendOfHenryFord-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 430">: 430 </span></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in 1940, with the requisitioning of between 100 and 200 French <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">POWs</a> to work as slave laborers, <i><a href="/wiki/Ford_Germany" title="Ford Germany">Ford-Werke</a></i> contravened Article 31 of the 1929 <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Convention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Limited" title="Rolls-Royce Limited">Rolls-Royce</a> sought a U.S. manufacturer as an additional source for the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Merlin</a> engine (as fitted to <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire" title="Supermarine Spitfire">Spitfire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hurricane</a> fighters), Ford first agreed to do so and then <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin#Packard_V-1650" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">reneged</a>. He "lined up behind the war effort" when the U.S. entered in December 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> His support of the American war effort, however, was problematic.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Willow_Run">Willow Run</span></h4>
<p>Before the U.S. entered the war, responding to President Roosevelt's call in December 1940 for the "Great Arsenal of Democracy", Ford directed the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> to construct a vast new purpose-built aircraft factory at <a href="/wiki/Willow_Run" title="Willow Run">Willow Run</a> near Detroit, Michigan. Ford broke ground on Willow Run in the spring of 1941, B-24 component production began in May 1942, and the first complete <a href="/wiki/Consolidated_B-24_Liberator" title="Consolidated B-24 Liberator">B-24</a> came off the line in October 1942. At 3,500,000 sq ft (330,000 m<sup>2</sup>), it was the largest assembly line in the world at the time. At its peak in 1944, the Willow Run plant produced 650 B-24s per month, and by 1945 Ford was completing each B-24 in eighteen hours, with one rolling off the assembly line every 58 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willowrun_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willowrun-65">[65]</a></sup> Ford produced 9,000 B-24s at Willow Run, half of the 18,000 total B-24s produced during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willowrun_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Willowrun-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LegendOfHenryFord_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-LegendOfHenryFord-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 430">: 430 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Edsel.27s_death"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edsel's_death">Edsel's death</span></h4>
<p>When Edsel Ford died of cancer in 1943, aged only 49, Henry Ford nominally resumed control of the company, but a series of strokes in the late 1930s had left him increasingly debilitated, and his mental ability was fading. Ford was increasingly sidelined, and others made decisions in his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The company was controlled by a handful of senior executives led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Sorensen" title="Charles Sorensen">Charles Sorensen</a>, an important engineer and production executive at Ford; and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Bennett" title="Harry Bennett">Harry Bennett</a>, the chief of Ford's Service Unit, Ford's paramilitary force that spied on, and enforced discipline upon, Ford employees. Ford grew jealous of the publicity Sorensen received and forced Sorensen out in 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Ford's incompetence led to discussions in Washington about how to restore the company, whether by wartime government fiat, or by instigating a coup among executives and directors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956pp324–333_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956pp324–333-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_out">Forced out</span></h4>
<p>Nothing happened until 1945 when, with bankruptcy a serious risk, Ford's wife Clara and Edsel's widow Eleanor confronted him and demanded he cede control of the company to his grandson <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford_II" title="Henry Ford II">Henry Ford II</a>. They threatened to sell off their stock, which amounted to three quarters of the company's total shares, if he refused. Ford was reportedly infuriated, but had no choice but to give in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Maybe an less than perferct RS (March 2014)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The young man took over and, as his first act of business, fired Harry Bennett.
</p>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title"><a href="/wiki/Category:Antisemitic_publications" title="Category:Antisemitic publications">Antisemitic publications</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content hlist" style="font-style:italic;">

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title"><a href="/wiki/Anti-antisemitism" title="Anti-antisemitism">Opposition</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content hlist">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="padding-top:0.15em;">
<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png"
