//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Antisemitism" title="Category:Antisemitism">Category</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Antisemitism_sidebar" title="Template:Antisemitism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Antisemitism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Antisemitism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Antisemitism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>All his life, Ford was a conspiracy theorist, as he and his writers drew on a long European tradition of false allegations against Jews. Ford claimed that Jewish internationalism posed a threat to traditional American values, which he deeply believed were at risk in the modern world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Part of his racist and antisemitic legacy includes the funding of square-dancing in American schools because he hated jazz and associated its creation with Jewish people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-72">[72]</a></sup> In 1920 Ford wrote, "If fans wish to know the trouble with American baseball they have it in three words—too much Jew."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyblueprint1_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyblueprint1-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1918, Ford's closest aide and private secretary, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_G._Liebold" title="Ernest G. Liebold">Ernest G. Liebold</a>, purchased an obscure weekly newspaper for Ford, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dearborn_Independent" title="The Dearborn Independent">The Dearborn Independent</a></i>. The <i>Independent</i> ran for eight years, from 1920 until 1927, with Liebold as editor. Every Ford franchise nationwide had to carry the paper and distribute it to its customers. With around 700,000 readers of his newspaper, Ford emerged as "a respected spokesman for right-wing extremism and religious prejudice.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="443" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg/170px-1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg/255px-1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg/340px-1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1920_International_Jew_reprint_from_Dearborn_Independent.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ford publication <i><a href="/wiki/The_International_Jew" title="The International Jew">The International Jew, the World's Foremost Problem</a></i>. Articles from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dearborn_Independent" title="The Dearborn Independent">The Dearborn Independent</a></i>, 1920</div></div></div>
<p>In Germany, Ford's antisemitic articles from <i>The Dearborn Independent</i> were issued in four volumes, cumulatively titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_International_Jew" title="The International Jew">The International Jew, the World's Foremost Problem</a></i> published by <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Fritsch" title="Theodor Fritsch">Theodor Fritsch</a>, founder of several antisemitic parties and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_(Weimar_Republic)" title="Reichstag (Weimar Republic)">Reichstag</a>. In a letter written in 1924, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> described Ford as "one of our most valuable, important, and witty fighters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Ford is the only American mentioned favorably in <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i>, although he is only mentioned twice:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> wrote, "only a single great man, Ford, [who], to [the Jews'] fury, still maintains full independence ... [from] the controlling masters of the producers in a nation of one hundred and twenty millions." Speaking in 1931 to a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit_News" title="Detroit News">Detroit News</a></i> reporter, Hitler said he regarded Ford as his "inspiration", explaining his reason for keeping Ford's life-size portrait next to his desk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-77">[77]</a></sup> Steven Watts wrote that Hitler "revered" Ford, proclaiming that "I shall do my best to put his theories into practice in Germany", and modeling the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Beetle" title="Volkswagen Beetle">Volkswagen Beetle</a>, the people's car, on the Model T.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Max Wallace has stated, "History records that ... Adolf Hitler was an ardent Anti-Semite before he ever read Ford's <i>The International Jew</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Under Ford, the newspaper also reprinted the <a href="/wiki/Antisemitic_canard" title="Antisemitic canard">antisemitic fabricated text</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protocols_of_the_Elders_of_Zion" title="The Protocols of the Elders of Zion">The Protocols of the Elders of Zion</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 1, 1924, Ford received <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurt_Ludecke" title="Kurt Ludecke">Kurt Ludecke</a>, a representative of Hitler, at home. Ludecke was introduced to Ford by <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Wagner" title="Siegfried Wagner">Siegfried Wagner</a> (son of the composer <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>) and his wife <a href="/wiki/Winifred_Wagner" title="Winifred Wagner">Winifred</a>, both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> sympathizers and antisemites. Ludecke asked Ford for a contribution to the Nazi cause, but was apparently refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford's articles were denounced by the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Defamation_League" title="Anti-Defamation League">Anti-Defamation League</a> (ADL). While these articles explicitly condemned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pogram" title="Pogram">pogroms</a> and violence against Jews, they blamed the Jews themselves for provoking them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> According to some trial testimony, none of this work was written by Ford, but he allowed his name to be used as an author. Friends and business associates have said they warned Ford about the contents of the <i>Independent</i> and that he probably never read the articles (he claimed he only read the headlines).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> On the other hand, court testimony in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libel" title="Libel">libel</a> suit, brought by one of the targets of the newspaper, alleged that Ford did know about the contents of the <i>Independent</i> in advance of publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>A libel lawsuit was brought by San Francisco lawyer and Jewish farm cooperative organizer <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Sapiro" title="Aaron Sapiro">Aaron Sapiro</a> in response to the antisemitic remarks, and led Ford to close the <i>Independent</i> in December 1927. News reports at the time quoted him as saying he was shocked by the content and unaware of its nature. During the trial, the editor of Ford's "Own Page", William Cameron, testified that Ford had nothing to do with the editorials even though they were under his byline. Cameron testified at the libel trial that he never discussed the content of the pages or sent them to Ford for his approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Investigative journalist <a href="/wiki/Max_Wallace" title="Max Wallace">Max Wallace</a> noted that "whatever credibility this absurd claim may have had was soon undermined when James M. Miller, a former <i>Dearborn Independent</i> employee, swore under oath that Ford had told him he intended to expose Sapiro."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Barkun" title="Michael Barkun">Michael Barkun</a> observed: "That Cameron would have continued to publish such anti-Semitic material without Ford's explicit instructions seemed unthinkable to those who knew both men. Mrs. Stanley Ruddiman, a Ford family intimate, remarked that "I don't think Mr. Cameron ever wrote anything for publication without Mr. Ford's approval."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> According to Spencer Blakeslee, "[t]he ADL mobilized prominent Jews and non-Jews to publicly oppose Ford's message. They formed a coalition of Jewish groups for the same purpose and raised constant objections in the Detroit press. Before leaving his presidency early in 1921, <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> joined other leading Americans in a statement that rebuked Ford and others for their antisemitic campaign. A boycott against Ford products by Jews and liberal Christians also had an impact, and Ford shut down the paper in 1927, recanting his views in a public letter to <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Livingston" title="Sigmund Livingston">Sigmund Livingston</a>, president of the ADL."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Wallace also found that Ford's apology was likely, or at least partly, motivated by a business that was slumping as a result of his antisemitism, repelling potential buyers of Ford cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-6"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup> Up until the apology, a considerable number of dealers, who had been required to make sure that buyers of Ford cars received the <i>Independent</i>, bought up and destroyed copies of the newspaper rather than alienate customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_44-7"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford's 1927 apology was well received. "Four-fifths of the hundreds of letters addressed to Ford in July 1927 were from Jews, and almost without exception they praised the industrialist..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-88">[88]</a></sup> In January 1937, a Ford statement to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Detroit_Jewish_Chronicle" title="The Detroit Jewish Chronicle">The Detroit Jewish Chronicle</a></i> disavowed "any connection whatsoever with the publication in Germany of a book known as the <i>International Jew</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-88">[88]</a></sup> Ford, however, allegedly never signed the retraction and apology, which were written by others—rather, his signature was forged by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Bennett" title="Harry Bennett">Harry Bennett</a>—and Ford never actually recanted his antisemitic views, stating in 1940: "I hope to republish <i>The International Jew</i> again some time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pool_and_Pool_1978_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pool_and_Pool_1978-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1198" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png/170px-Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png/255px-Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png/340px-Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Service_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle" title="Grand Cross of the German Eagle">Grand Cross of the German Eagle</a>, an award bestowed on Ford by Nazi Germany</div></div></div>
<p>In July 1938, the German consul in Cleveland gave Ford, on his 75th birthday, the award of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Cross_of_the_German_Eagle" title="Grand Cross of the German Eagle">Grand Cross of the German Eagle</a>, the highest medal Nazi Germany could bestow on a foreigner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-WP-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_D._Mooney" title="James D. Mooney">James D. Mooney</a>, vice president of overseas operations for <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, received a similar medal, the Merit Cross of the German Eagle, First Class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-WP-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 7, 1942, Ford wrote another letter to Sigmund Livingston disclaiming direct or indirect support of "any agitation which would promote antagonism toward my Jewish fellow citizens". He concluded the letter with, "My sincere hope that now in this country and throughout the world when the war is finished, hatred of the Jews and hatred against any other racial or religious groups shall cease for all time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnstein_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnstein-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The distribution of <i>The International Jew</i> was halted in 1942 through legal action by Ford, despite complications from a lack of copyright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-88">[88]</a></sup> It is still banned in Germany. Extremist groups often recycle the material; it still appears on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antisemitic" title="Antisemitic">antisemitic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Nazi" title="Neo-Nazi">neo-Nazi</a> websites. Testifying at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg</a>, convicted <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Baldur_von_Schirach" title="Baldur von Schirach">Baldur von Schirach</a> who, in his role as <i><a href="/wiki/Gauleiter" title="Gauleiter">Gauleiter</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, deported 65,000 Jews to camps in Poland, stated: "The decisive anti-Semitic book I was reading and the book that influenced my comrades was ... that book by Henry Ford, <i><a href="/wiki/The_International_Jew" title="The International Jew">The International Jew</a></i>. I read it and became anti-Semitic. The book made a great influence on myself and my friends because we saw in Henry Ford the representative of success and also the representative of a progressive social policy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Robert Lacey wrote in <i>Ford: The Men and the Machines</i> that a close Willow Run associate of Ford reported that when he was shown newsreel footage of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a>, he "was confronted with the atrocities which finally and unanswerably laid bare the bestiality of the prejudice to which he contributed, he collapsed with a stroke – his last and most serious."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Ford had suffered previous strokes and his final cerebral hemorrhage occurred in 1947 at age 83.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-1947.04.08_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-1947.04.08-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_business">International business</span></h2>
<p>Ford's philosophy was one of <a href="/wiki/Economic_nationalism" title="Economic nationalism">economic independence</a> for the United States. His <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford_River_Rouge_Complex" title="Ford River Rouge Complex">River Rouge Plant</a> became the world's largest industrial complex, pursuing <a href="/wiki/Vertical_integration" title="Vertical integration">vertical integration</a> to such an extent that it could produce its own steel. Ford's goal was to produce a vehicle from scratch without reliance on foreign trade. He believed in the global expansion of his company. He believed that international trade and cooperation led to international peace, and he used the assembly line process and production of the Model T to demonstrate it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>He opened Ford assembly plants in Britain and Canada in 1911, and soon became the biggest automotive producer in those countries. In 1912, Ford cooperated with <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Agnelli" title="Giovanni Agnelli">Giovanni Agnelli</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fiat" title="Fiat">Fiat</a> to launch the first Italian automotive assembly plants. The first plants in Germany were built in the 1920s with the encouragement of <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> and the Commerce Department, which agreed with Ford's theory that international trade was essential to world peace and reduced the chance of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In the 1920s, Ford also opened plants in Australia, India, and France, and by 1929, he had successful dealerships on six continents. Ford experimented with a commercial rubber plantation in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon</a> jungle called <a href="/wiki/Fordl%C3%A2ndia" title="Fordlândia">Fordlândia</a>; it was one of his few failures.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="707" data-file-width="454" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5b/Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg/170px-Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5b/Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg/255px-Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5b/Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg/340px-Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ford_Bron_Mezhlauk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After signing the contract for technical assistance in building Nizhny Novgorod (Gorky) Automobile Plant. Dearborn, Mich., May 31, 1929. Left to right, <a href="/wiki/Valery_Mezhlauk" title="Valery Mezhlauk">Valery I. Mezhlauk</a>, Vice Chairman of VSNKh; Henry Ford; <a href="/wiki/Saul_Bron" title="Saul Bron">Saul G. Bron</a>, President of Amtorg.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1929, Ford made an agreement with the Soviets to provide technical aid over nine years in building the <a href="/wiki/GAZ" title="GAZ">first Soviet automobile plant (GAZ)</a> near <a href="/wiki/Nizhny_Novgorod" title="Nizhny Novgorod">Nizhny Novgorod (Gorky)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> (an additional contract for construction of the plant was signed with The Austin Company on August 23, 1929).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The contract involved the purchase of $30,000,000 worth of knocked-down Ford cars and trucks for assembly during the first four years of the plant's operation, after which the plant would gradually switch to Soviet-made components. Ford sent his engineers and technicians to the Soviet Union to help install the equipment and train the workforce, while over a hundred Soviet engineers and technicians were stationed at Ford's plants in Detroit and Dearborn "for the purpose of learning the methods and practice of manufacture and assembly in the Company's plants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Said Ford: "No matter where industry prospers, whether in India or China, or Russia, the more profit there will be for everyone, including us. All the world is bound to catch some good from it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1932, Ford was manufacturing one-third of the world's automobiles. It set up numerous subsidiaries that sold or assembled the Ford cars and trucks:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 18em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ford_Australia" title="Ford Australia">Ford of Australia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ford_of_Britain" title="Ford of Britain">Ford of Britain</a></li>
<li>Ford of Argentina</li>
<li>Ford of Brazil</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company_of_Canada" title="Ford Motor Company of Canada">Ford of Canada</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ford_of_Europe" title="Ford of Europe">Ford of Europe</a></li>
<li>Ford India</li>
<li>Ford South Africa</li>
<li>Ford Mexico</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company_Philippines" title="Ford Motor Company Philippines">Ford Philippines</a></li></ul>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400,_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="568" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400%2C_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400%2C_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400%2C_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400%2C_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400%2C_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400%2C_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10400,_Henry_Ford_in_Deutschland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry Ford in Germany; September 1930</div></div></div>
<p>Ford's image transfixed Europeans, especially the Germans, arousing the "fear of some, the infatuation of others, and the fascination among all".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Germans who discussed "Fordism" often believed that it represented something quintessentially American. They saw the size, tempo, standardization, and philosophy of production demonstrated at the Ford Works as a national service—an "American thing" that represented the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_States" title="Culture of the United States">culture of the United States</a>. Both supporters and critics insisted that Fordism epitomized American capitalist development, and that the auto industry was the key to understanding economic and social relations in the United States. As one German explained, "Automobiles have so completely changed the American's mode of life that today one can hardly imagine being without a car. It is difficult to remember what life was like before Mr. Ford began preaching his doctrine of salvation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> For many Germans, Ford embodied the essence of successful Americanism.
</p><p>In <i>My Life and Work</i>, Ford predicted that if greed, racism, and short-sightedness could be overcome, then economic and technological development throughout the world would progress to the point that international trade would no longer be based on (what today would be called) colonial or <a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">neocolonial</a> models and would truly benefit all peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_Crowther_1922_pp242-244_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford_Crowther_1922_pp242-244-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Racing">Racing</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999,_1902.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1459" data-file-width="2568" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999%2C_1902.jpg/220px-Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999%2C_1902.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999%2C_1902.jpg/330px-Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999%2C_1902.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999%2C_1902.jpg/440px-Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999%2C_1902.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Ford_and_Barney_Oldfield_with_Old_999,_1902.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ford (standing) launched <a href="/wiki/Barney_Oldfield" title="Barney Oldfield">Barney Oldfield</a>'s career in 1902</div></div></div>
<p>Ford maintained an interest in auto racing from 1901 to 1913 and began his involvement in the sport as both a constructor and a driver, later turning the wheel over to hired drivers. He entered stripped-down <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Model_T" title="Model T">Model Ts</a> in races, finishing first (although later disqualified) in an "ocean-to-ocean" (across the United States) race in 1909, and setting a one-mile (1.6 km) oval speed record at Detroit Fairgrounds in 1911 with driver Frank Kulick. In 1913, Ford attempted to enter a reworked Model T in the <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_500" title="Indianapolis 500">Indianapolis 500</a> but was told rules required the addition of another 1,000 pounds (450 kg) to the car before it could qualify. Ford dropped out of the race and soon thereafter dropped out of racing permanently, citing dissatisfaction with the sport's rules, demands on his time by the booming production of the Model T, and his low opinion of racing as a worthwhile activity.
</p><p>In <i>My Life and Work</i> Ford speaks (briefly) of racing in a rather dismissive tone, as something that is not at all a good measure of automobiles in general. He describes himself as someone who raced only because in the 1890s through 1910s, one had to race because prevailing ignorance held that racing was the way to prove the worth of an automobile. Ford did not agree. But he was determined that as long as this was the definition of success (flawed though the definition was), then his cars would be the best that there were at racing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford_Crowther_1922_p50_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford_Crowther_1922_p50-105">[105]</a></sup> Throughout the book, he continually returns to ideals such as transportation, production efficiency, affordability, reliability, fuel efficiency, economic prosperity, and the automation of drudgery in farming and industry, but rarely mentions, and rather belittles, the idea of merely going fast from point A to point B.
</p><p>Nevertheless, Ford did make quite an impact on auto racing during his racing years, and he was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Motorsports_Hall_of_Fame_of_America" title="Motorsports Hall of Fame of America">Motorsports Hall of Fame of America</a> in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_career_and_death">Later career and death</span></h2>
<p>When Edsel Ford, President of Ford Motor Company, died of cancer in May 1943, the elderly and ailing Henry Ford decided to assume the presidency. By this point, Ford, nearing 80 years old, had had several cardiovascular events (variously cited as heart attacks or strokes) and was mentally inconsistent, suspicious, and generally no longer fit for such immense responsibilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956pp100_266_et_al_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956pp100_266_et_al-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the directors did not want to see him as president. But for the previous 20 years, though he had long been without any official executive title, he had always had <i>de facto</i> control over the company; the board and the management had never seriously defied him, and this time was no different. The directors elected him,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956pp325–326_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956pp325–326-108">[108]</a></sup> and he served until the end of the war. During this period the company began to decline, losing more than $10 million a month ($156,600,000 today). The administration of President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt">Franklin Roosevelt</a> had been considering a government takeover of the company in order to ensure continued war production,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorensen1956pp324–333_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sorensen1956pp324–333-68">[68]</a></sup> but the idea never progressed.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG/220px-Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG/330px-Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG/440px-Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Ford_grave_Ford_Cemetery_Detroit.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ford grave, Ford Cemetery</div></div></div>
<p>His health failing, Ford ceded the company presidency to his grandson <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford_II" title="Henry Ford II">Henry Ford II</a> in September 1945 and retired. He died on April 7, 1947, of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemorrhage" title="Cerebral hemorrhage">cerebral hemorrhage</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fair_Lane" title="Fair Lane">Fair Lane</a>, his estate in Dearborn, at the age of 83. A public viewing was held at Greenfield Village where up to 5,000 people per hour filed past the casket. Funeral services were held in Detroit's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_Church_of_St._Paul,_Detroit" title="Cathedral Church of St. Paul, Detroit">Cathedral Church of St. Paul</a> and he was buried in the Ford Cemetery in Detroit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-1947.04.08_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-AP-1947.04.08-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_interests">Personal interests</span></h2>
<p>A compendium of short biographies of famous <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasons</a>, published by a Freemason lodge, lists Ford as a member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denslow2004p62_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denslow2004p62-110">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Lodge_of_New_York" title="Grand Lodge of New York">Grand Lodge of New York</a> confirms that Ford was a Freemason, and was raised in Palestine Lodge No. 357, Detroit, in 1894. When he received the 33rd degree of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Rite" title="Scottish Rite">Scottish Rite</a> in 1940, he said, "Masonry is the best balance wheel the United States has."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1923, Ford's pastor, and head of his sociology department, Episcopal minister Samuel S. Marquis, claimed that Ford believed, or "once believed," in <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford published an anti-smoking book, circulated to youth in 1914, called <i>The Case Against the Little White Slaver</i>, which documented many dangers of cigarette smoking attested to by many researchers and luminaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> At the time, smoking was ubiquitous and not yet widely associated with health problems, making Ford's opposition to cigarettes unusual.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interest_in_materials_science_and_engineering">Interest in materials science and engineering</span></h3>
<p>Henry Ford had a long-held interest in <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science and engineering</a>. He enthusiastically described his company's adoption of vanadium steel alloys and subsequent metallurgic R&amp;D work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford1922pp18_65-67_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford1922pp18_65-67-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford also had a long-standing interest in plastics developed from agricultural products, particularly <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybeans</a>. He cultivated a relationship with <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Carver" title="George Washington Carver">George Washington Carver</a> for this purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Soybean-based plastics were used in Ford automobiles throughout the 1930s in plastic parts such as car horns, in paint and other components. The project culminated in 1942, when Ford <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybean_Car" title="Soybean Car">patented an automobile made almost entirely of plastic</a>, attached to a tubular welded frame. It weighed 30% less than a steel car and was said to be able to withstand blows ten times greater than steel. It ran on grain alcohol (<a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>) instead of gasoline. The design never caught on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis1995_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis1995-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ford was interested in <a href="/wiki/Engineered_wood" title="Engineered wood">engineered woods</a> ("Better wood can be made than is grown"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford1922p281_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford1922p281-119">[119]</a></sup>) (at this time plywood and particle board were little more than experimental ideas); <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">corn as a fuel source</a>, via both corn oil and ethanol;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford1922pp275-276_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford1922pp275-276-120">[120]</a></sup> and the potential uses of cotton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford1922p281_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ford1922p281-119">[119]</a></sup> Ford was instrumental in developing charcoal <a href="/wiki/Briquette" title="Briquette">briquets</a>, under the brand name "<a href="/wiki/Kingsford_(charcoal)" title="Kingsford (charcoal)">Kingsford</a>". His brother-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Edward_G._Kingsford" title="Edward G. Kingsford">Edward G. Kingsford</a>, used wood scraps from the Ford factory to make the briquets.
</p><p>In 1927, Ford partnered with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvey_Samuel_Firestone" title="Harvey Samuel Firestone">Harvey Samuel Firestone</a> (each contributing $25,000) to create the Edison Botanic Research Corp. in Fort Myers, Florida to seek a native source of rubber.
</p><p>Ford was a <a href="/wiki/List_of_prolific_inventors" title="List of prolific inventors">prolific inventor</a> and was awarded 161 U.S. patents.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Florida_and_Georgia_residences_and_community">Florida and Georgia residences and community</span></h3>
<p>Ford had a vacation residence in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myers,_Florida" title="Fort Myers, Florida">Fort Myers, Florida</a>, next to that of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a>, which he bought in 1915 and used until approximately 1930. It still stands today as a museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>He also had a vacation home (known today as the "Ford Plantation") in <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Hill,_Georgia" title="Richmond Hill, Georgia">Richmond Hill</a>, Georgia, which is now a private community. Ford started buying land in this area and eventually owned 70,000 acres (110 square miles) there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> In 1936, Ford broke ground for a beautiful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_revival" title="Greek revival">Greek revival</a> style mansion on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Ogeechee_River" title="Ogeechee River">Ogeechee River</a> on the site of a 1730s plantation. The grand house, made of Savannah-gray brick, had marble steps, air conditioning, and an <a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">elevator</a>. It sat on 55 acres (22 ha) of manicured lawns and flowering gardens. The house became the center of social gatherings with visitations by the Vanderbilts, Rockefellers, and the DuPonts. It remains the centerpiece of The Ford Plantation today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Ford converted the 1870s-era rice mill into his personal research laboratory and powerhouse and constructed a tunnel from there to the new home, providing it with steam. He contributed substantially to the community, building a chapel and schoolhouse and employing numerous local residents.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preserving_Americana">Preserving Americana</span></h3>
<p>Ford had an interest in "<a href="/wiki/Americana_(culture)" title="Americana (culture)">Americana</a>". In the 1920s, he began work to turn <a href="/wiki/Sudbury,_Massachusetts" title="Sudbury, Massachusetts">Sudbury</a>, Massachusetts, into a themed historical village. He moved the schoolhouse supposedly referred to in the "<a href="/wiki/Mary_Had_a_Little_Lamb" title="Mary Had a Little Lamb">Mary Had a Little Lamb</a>" nursery rhyme from <a href="/wiki/Sterling,_Massachusetts" title="Sterling, Massachusetts">Sterling</a>, Massachusetts, and purchased the historic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wayside_Inn_(Sudbury)" title="Wayside Inn (Sudbury)">Wayside Inn</a>. The historical village plan never came to fruition. He repeated the concept of collecting historic structures with the creation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenfield_Village" title="Greenfield Village">Greenfield Village</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dearborn,_Michigan" title="Dearborn, Michigan">Dearborn</a>, Michigan. It may have inspired the creation of <a href="/wiki/Old_Sturbridge_Village" title="Old Sturbridge Village">Old Sturbridge Village</a> as well. About the same time, he began collecting materials for <a href="/wiki/The_Henry_Ford" title="The Henry Ford">his museum</a>, which had a theme of practical technology. It was opened in 1929 as the Edison Institute. The museum has been greatly modernized and is still open today.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1438" data-file-width="2568" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg/220px-Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg/330px-Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg/440px-Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mr_and_Mrs_Henry_Ford_in_his_first_car.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry and Clara Ford in his first car, the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Quadricycle" title="Ford Quadricycle">Ford Quadricycle</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li>In <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World">Brave New World</a></i> (1932), society is organized on "Fordist" lines, the years are dated A.F. or Anno Ford ("In the Year of Ford"), and the expression "My Ford" is used instead of "My Lord". The Christian cross is replaced with a capital "T" for Model-T.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Upton_Sinclair" title="Upton Sinclair">Upton Sinclair</a> created a fictional description of Ford in the 1937 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Flivver_King" title="The Flivver King">The Flivver King</a></i>.</li>
<li>Symphonic composer <a href="/wiki/Ferde_Grof%C3%A9" title="Ferde Grofé">Ferde Grofé</a> composed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tone_poem" title="Tone poem">tone poem</a> in Henry Ford's honor (1938).</li>
<li>Ford appears as a character in several historical novels, notably <a href="/wiki/E._L._Doctorow" title="E. L. Doctorow">E. L. Doctorow</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ragtime_(novel)" title="Ragtime (novel)">Ragtime</a></i> (1975), and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Powers" title="Richard Powers">Richard Powers</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Farmers_on_Their_Way_to_a_Dance" title="Three Farmers on Their Way to a Dance">Three Farmers on Their Way to a Dance</a></i> (1985).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ford, his family, and his company were the subjects of a <a href="/wiki/Ford:_The_Man_and_the_Machine" title="Ford: The Man and the Machine">1987 film</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Robertson" title="Cliff Robertson">Cliff Robertson</a>  and  <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ironside" title="Michael Ironside">Michael Ironside</a>, based on the 1986 biography <i>Ford: The Man and the Machine</i> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lacey" title="Robert Lacey">Robert Lacey</a>.</li>
<li>In the 2004 alternative history novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Plot_Against_America" title="The Plot Against America">The Plot Against America</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Roth" title="Philip Roth">Philip Roth</a> features Ford as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Secretary of the Interior</a> in a fictional <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> presidential administration after Lindbergh's victory over Roosevelt in the 1940 presidential election. The novel draws heavily on the administration's antisemitism and <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationism</a> as a catalyst for its plot.
<ul><li>In the 2020 <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> <a href="/wiki/The_Plot_Against_America_(miniseries)" title="The Plot Against America (miniseries)">adapted miniseries of the same name</a>, Ford is portrayed by actor Ed Moran.</li></ul></li>
<li>Ford appears as a Great Builder in the 2008 strategy video game <i><a href="/wiki/Civilization_Revolution" title="Civilization Revolution">Civilization Revolution</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the fictional history of the <i><a href="/wiki/Assassin%27s_Creed" title="Assassin's Creed">Assassin's Creed</a></i> video game franchise, Ford is portrayed as having been a major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Templar" title="Templar">Templar</a> influence on the events of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_recognition">Honors and recognition</span></h2>
<ul><li>In December 1999, Ford was among 18 included in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallup%27s_List_of_Widely_Admired_People" title="Gallup's List of Widely Admired People">Gallup's List of Widely Admired People of the 20th Century</a>, from a poll conducted of the American people.</li>
<li>In 1928, Ford was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Institute" title="Franklin Institute">Franklin Institute</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Cresson_Medal" title="Elliott Cresson Medal">Elliott Cresson Medal</a>.</li>
<li>In 1938, Ford was awarded Nazi Germany's <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_German_Eagle" title="Order of the German Eagle">Grand Cross of the German Eagle</a>, a medal given to foreigners sympathetic to Nazism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> honored Ford with a <a href="/wiki/Prominent_Americans_series" title="Prominent Americans series">Prominent Americans series</a> (1965–1978) 12¢ postage stamp.</li>
<li>He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_Hall_of_Fame" title="Automotive Hall of Fame">Automotive Hall of Fame</a> in 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blade_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blade-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHF-Ford_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHF-Ford-131">[131]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1975, Ford was posthumously inducted into the Junior Achievement <a href="/wiki/U.S._Business_Hall_of_Fame" title="U.S. Business Hall of Fame">U.S. Business Hall of Fame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1985, he was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/National_Aviation_Hall_of_Fame" title="National Aviation Hall of Fame">National Aviation Hall of Fame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li>He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Motorsports_Hall_of_Fame_of_America" title="Motorsports Hall of Fame of America">Motorsports Hall of Fame of America</a> in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSHoF_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSHoF-134">[134]</a></sup></li></ul>

James (October 10, 2014). <a class="external text" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/national/religion/the-dark-legacy-of-henry-fords-anti-semitism-commentary/2014/10/10/c95b7df2-509d-11e4-877c-335b53ffe736_story.html" rel="nofollow">"The dark legacy of Henry Ford's anti-Semitism"</a>. <i>The Washington Post</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 14,</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Washington+Post&amp;rft.atitle=The+dark+legacy+of+Henry+Ford%27s+anti-Semitism&amp;rft.date=2014-10-10&amp;rft.aulast=Rudin&amp;rft.aufirst=A.+James&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.washingtonpost.com%2Fnational%2Freligion%2Fthe-dark-legacy-of-henry-fords-anti-semitism-commentary%2F2014%2F10%2F10%2Fc95b7df2-509d-11e4-877c-335b53ffe736_story.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHenry+Ford"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Max Wallace. <i>The American Axis: Henry Ford, Charles Lindbergh, and the Rise of the Third Reich</i>, (Macmillan, 2004), pp. 50–54, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-312-33531-8" title="Special:BookSources/0-312-33531-8">0-312-33531-8</a>. Years later, in 1977, Winifred claimed that Ford had told her that he had helped finance Hitler. This anecdote is the suggestion that Ford made a contribution. The company has always denied that any contribution was made, and no documentary evidence has ever been found (<i>ibid</i> p. 54). However, according to a captured Nazi document, the German subsidiary of Ford made a personal present to Hitler of 35,000 Reichsmarks in honor of his 50th birthday, in April 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-WP-77">[77]</a></sup> See also <a href="/wiki/Neil_Baldwin_(writer)" title="Neil Baldwin (writer)">Neil Baldwin</a>, <i>Henry Ford and the Jews: The Mass Production of Hate</i> (Public Affairs, 2002), pp. 185–89, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-58648-163-0" title="Special:BookSources/1-58648-163-0">1-58648-163-0</a>.</span>
